# Title: Congress and the American Political System I: A Comprehensive Study Guide

## Chapter 19: The Role of Congress in Foreign Policy




### Introduction

The role of Congress in immigration policy is a complex and multifaceted one. As the legislative branch of the United States government, Congress plays a crucial role in shaping and implementing immigration policies. This chapter will delve into the various aspects of Congress's role in immigration, including its power to make laws, its oversight of immigration agencies, and its influence on the public discourse surrounding immigration.

Congress's power to make laws is a fundamental aspect of its role in immigration policy. The Constitution grants Congress the power to establish a uniform rule of naturalization, which is the process by which a foreign national becomes a U.S. citizen. This power allows Congress to set the criteria for who can become a citizen, as well as the process for obtaining citizenship.

In addition to its lawmaking power, Congress also plays a crucial role in overseeing immigration agencies. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and the Department of Justice (DOJ) are two key agencies responsible for enforcing immigration laws. Congress has the power to fund and oversee these agencies, and can use its oversight powers to investigate and address any issues or concerns related to immigration policy.

Finally, Congress also plays a significant role in shaping public discourse surrounding immigration. As the branch of government closest to the people, Congress is often the face of immigration policy for the general public. Its actions and decisions can greatly influence public opinion and perception of immigration, and can have a significant impact on the political landscape surrounding this issue.

In this chapter, we will explore these aspects of Congress's role in immigration policy in more detail, providing a comprehensive understanding of its influence and impact on this important issue.




### Subsection: 19.1 The Department of Homeland Security

The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) is a cabinet-level department of the United States federal government responsible for public safety, security, and disaster management. It was created in response to the 9/11 terrorist attacks as a way to better coordinate and manage the government's efforts to prevent and respond to terrorist threats. The DHS is headed by the Secretary of Homeland Security, who is appointed by the President and confirmed by the Senate.

The DHS is responsible for a wide range of functions, including border security, immigration enforcement, disaster management, and cybersecurity. It also includes several subordinate agencies, including the U.S. Customs and Border Protection (CBP), U.S. Immigration and Customs Enforcement (ICE), and the U.S. Coast Guard.

## Structure

The DHS is headed by the Secretary of Homeland Security, who is assisted by the Deputy Secretary. The department contains the components listed below.

### List of subordinate agencies

Passports for U.S. citizens are issued by the U.S. Department of State, not the Department of Homeland Security.

Advisory groups:

Other components:

In an August 5, 2002, speech, President Bush said: "We are fighting ... to secure freedom in the homeland." Prior to the creation of DHS, U.S. Presidents had referred to the U.S. as "the nation" or "the republic" and to its internal policies as "domestic". Also unprecedented was the use, from 2002, of the phrase "the homeland" by White House spokespeople.

## National Terrorism Advisory System

In 2011, the Department of Homeland Security phased out the old Homeland Security Advisory System, replacing it with a two-level National Terrorism Advisory System. The system has two types of advisories: alerts and bulletins. NTAS bulletins permit the secretary to communicate critical terrorism information that, while not necessarily indicative of a specific threat against the United States, can reach homeland security partners or the public quickly, thereby allowing recipients to implement necessary protective measures. Alerts are issued when there is specific and credible information of a terrorist threat against the United States. Alerts have two levels: elevated and imminent. An elevated alert is issued when there is credible information about an attack but only general information about timing or a target. An Imminent Alert is issued when the threat is very specific and impending in the very near term.

On March 12, 2002, the Homeland Security Advisory System, a color-coded terrorism risk advisory scale, was created as the result of a Presidential Directive to provide a "comprehensive and effective means to disseminate information regarding the risk of terrorist acts to federal, state, and local governments and the American people." The system was designed to provide a clear, consistent, and understandable means of conveying information about the risk of terrorist attacks to the public. The system was composed of five color-coded levels:

- Green: Low risk of terrorist attack
- Blue: Guarded risk of terrorist attack
- Yellow: Elevated risk of terrorist attack
- Orange: High risk of terrorist attack
- Red: Severe risk of terrorist attack

The system was criticized for its lack of specificity and for creating unnecessary fear and panic among the public. As a result, it was replaced by the National Terrorism Advisory System in 2011.





### Subsection: 19.2 The Immigration and Nationality Act

The Immigration and Nationality Act (INA) is a comprehensive federal law that governs immigration to the United States. It was first enacted in 1952 and has been amended numerous times since then. The INA sets out the procedures for obtaining visas, the requirements for naturalization, and the grounds for deportation. It also establishes the principles of family reunification and diversity immigration.

## Procedures for Obtaining Visas

The INA sets out the procedures for obtaining visas, which are documents that allow foreign nationals to enter the United States. The process begins with the filing of a petition by a U.S. citizen or lawful permanent resident on behalf of the foreign national. The petition is then reviewed by the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS), which determines whether it meets the requirements of the INA. If the petition is approved, the foreign national may apply for a visa at a U.S. consulate abroad.

## Requirements for Naturalization

The INA sets out the requirements for naturalization, which is the process by which a foreign national becomes a U.S. citizen. The requirements include a period of continuous residence in the United States, good moral character, knowledge of English and U.S. history and government, and an oath of allegiance to the United States. The INA also provides for exceptions to these requirements in certain circumstances, such as for spouses of U.S. citizens.

## Grounds for Deportation

The INA sets out the grounds for deportation, which is the process by which a foreign national is removed from the United States. The grounds for deportation include criminal convictions, fraud in obtaining immigration benefits, and failure to maintain lawful status. The INA also provides for certain forms of relief from deportation, such as cancellation of removal and adjustment of status.

## Principles of Family Reunification and Diversity Immigration

The INA establishes the principles of family reunification and diversity immigration. Family reunification allows U.S. citizens and lawful permanent residents to sponsor certain family members for immigration to the United States. Diversity immigration, on the other hand, is a lottery system that allows a certain number of immigrants from countries with low rates of immigration to the United States to obtain visas.

## Amendments to the INA

The INA has been amended numerous times since its enactment in 1952. Some of the major amendments include the Immigration Act of 1965, which eliminated the national origins quota system and established the principles of family reunification and diversity immigration; the Immigration Reform and Control Act of 1986, which provided amnesty for certain undocumented immigrants and established the Immigration and Naturalization Service (INS); and the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, which increased border security and established the Department of Homeland Security.




### Subsection: 19.1b The Politics of Immigration Policy in Congress

The politics of immigration policy in Congress have been a contentious issue for decades. The Immigration and Nationality Act (INA) has been a key piece of legislation in this debate, with its provisions and amendments shaping the landscape of immigration in the United States.

## The Role of Congress in Immigration Policy

Congress plays a crucial role in immigration policy, as it is responsible for creating and amending the laws that govern immigration. The INA, for example, was first enacted in 1952 and has been amended numerous times since then. These amendments have been driven by political debates and shifts in public opinion on immigration.

## The Politics of Immigration Amendments

The politics of immigration amendments have been shaped by a variety of factors, including economic concerns, security concerns, and humanitarian concerns. For example, the 1965 Immigration Act, which eliminated the national origins quota system and established a new system based on family reunification and diversity, was a response to changing economic and social conditions in the United States.

## The Role of Congress in Immigration Enforcement

Congress also plays a key role in immigration enforcement, as it sets the policies and funding for immigration enforcement agencies such as U.S. Immigration and Customs Enforcement (ICE) and U.S. Customs and Border Protection (CBP). The INA provides the legal framework for immigration enforcement, including the grounds for deportation and the procedures for obtaining visas.

## The Politics of Immigration Enforcement

The politics of immigration enforcement have been shaped by a variety of factors, including public safety concerns, economic concerns, and humanitarian concerns. For example, the 1996 Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act, which increased funding for immigration enforcement and established new grounds for deportation, was a response to concerns about illegal immigration and its impact on public safety and the economy.

## The Role of Congress in Immigration Reform

Congress has also been a key player in immigration reform efforts, which aim to address the challenges and opportunities presented by immigration in the United States. These efforts have been driven by a variety of factors, including economic concerns, security concerns, and humanitarian concerns. For example, the 2013 Senate Immigration Reform Bill, which proposed a pathway to citizenship for undocumented immigrants and increased funding for border security, was a response to the changing demographics of the United States and the need for a comprehensive approach to immigration.

## The Politics of Immigration Reform

The politics of immigration reform have been shaped by a variety of factors, including political ideology, party politics, and public opinion. For example, the 2013 Senate Immigration Reform Bill faced significant opposition from conservative Republicans, who argued that it was too lenient on undocumented immigrants and did not adequately address border security concerns. This opposition ultimately led to the bill's failure to pass in the House of Representatives.

## The Role of Congress in Immigration Policy Today

Today, Congress continues to play a crucial role in immigration policy, as it is responsible for addressing the ongoing challenges and opportunities presented by immigration in the United States. The politics of immigration policy in Congress remain shaped by a variety of factors, including economic concerns, security concerns, and humanitarian concerns. As the United States continues to grapple with these issues, the role of Congress in immigration policy will remain a key factor in shaping the future of immigration in the United States.





#### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the role of Congress in immigration policy. We have seen how Congress has the power to make and change immigration laws, and how it has used this power to shape the immigration policies of the United States. We have also discussed the various factors that influence Congress's approach to immigration, including political ideology, public opinion, and economic considerations.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of Congress in the immigration process. As the branch of government responsible for making and changing laws, Congress plays a crucial role in determining who can enter the United States and under what conditions. Its decisions have a direct impact on the lives of millions of people, both within the United States and around the world.

Another important aspect of Congress's role in immigration is its oversight of the executive branch. As we have seen, Congress has the power to investigate and hold hearings on immigration policies and practices. This oversight is essential in ensuring that the executive branch is acting within the bounds of the law and in the best interests of the nation.

In conclusion, Congress plays a vital role in shaping immigration policy in the United States. Its power to make and change laws, its oversight of the executive branch, and its responsiveness to public opinion make it a key player in the immigration debate. As we continue to grapple with the complex issues surrounding immigration, it is crucial to understand the role of Congress and its impact on this important aspect of American life.

In conclusion, Congress plays a vital role in shaping immigration policy in the United States. Its power to make and change laws, its oversight of the executive branch, and its responsiveness to public opinion make it a key player in the immigration debate. As we continue to grapple with the complex issues surrounding immigration, it is crucial to understand the role of Congress and its impact on this important aspect of American life.

### Introduction

The role of Congress in criminal justice policy is a crucial aspect of the American political system. As the legislative branch of the government, Congress plays a significant role in shaping the laws and policies that govern the criminal justice system. This chapter will provide a comprehensive study guide to understanding the role of Congress in criminal justice policy, covering various topics such as the history of Congress' involvement in criminal justice, key legislation and debates, and the impact of Congress' decisions on the criminal justice system.

Congress' involvement in criminal justice policy dates back to the early days of the United States, with the first federal criminal laws being passed in the 1790s. Since then, Congress has played a crucial role in shaping the criminal justice system, passing laws and policies that have had a significant impact on the way crimes are investigated, prosecuted, and punished.

One of the key pieces of legislation that Congress has passed in the criminal justice system is the Federal Criminal Code, which outlines the federal crimes and their corresponding punishments. This code has been amended and updated numerous times over the years, reflecting the changing priorities and values of the nation.

In addition to passing laws, Congress also plays a role in overseeing the criminal justice system. This includes conducting oversight hearings, investigating allegations of misconduct, and funding various criminal justice agencies. Congress' oversight role is essential in ensuring that the criminal justice system operates fairly and effectively.

This chapter will also explore the impact of Congress' decisions on the criminal justice system. From the passing of landmark legislation to the funding of criminal justice agencies, Congress' actions have a significant impact on the way crimes are investigated, prosecuted, and punished in the United States.

In conclusion, the role of Congress in criminal justice policy is a crucial aspect of the American political system. This chapter will provide a comprehensive study guide to understanding this role, covering various topics and exploring the impact of Congress' decisions on the criminal justice system.

### Subsection: 20.1 The Department of Justice

The Department of Justice (DoJ) is a federal executive department responsible for upholding the law and defending the interests of the United States. It is headed by the Attorney General, who is nominated by the President and confirmed by the Senate. The DoJ plays a crucial role in the criminal justice system, as it is responsible for enforcing federal laws and prosecuting federal crimes.

#### The Role of the Department of Justice in Criminal Justice Policy

The DoJ plays a significant role in shaping criminal justice policy in the United States. It is responsible for enforcing federal laws and regulations, investigating and prosecuting federal crimes, and providing legal advice to the President and other federal agencies. The DoJ also plays a crucial role in shaping federal criminal law and policy through its involvement in the legislative process.

The DoJ's involvement in criminal justice policy extends beyond its role in enforcing federal laws. It also plays a key role in shaping state and local criminal justice policies through its partnerships and collaborations with state and local law enforcement agencies. This collaboration is essential in addressing complex criminal justice issues, such as drug trafficking, terrorism, and cybercrime.

#### The Department of Justice and Congress

The DoJ works closely with Congress to shape criminal justice policy. Congress has the power to pass laws and set policies that govern the criminal justice system, and the DoJ is responsible for enforcing these laws and policies. The DoJ provides legal advice and assistance to Congress in drafting and passing criminal justice legislation.

Congress also plays a crucial role in overseeing the DoJ's actions. It has the power to investigate and hold hearings to examine the DoJ's policies and actions. Congress also has the power to appropriate funding for the DoJ, giving it a significant influence over the department's operations.

#### The Department of Justice and the Criminal Justice System

The DoJ plays a crucial role in the criminal justice system, as it is responsible for enforcing federal laws and prosecuting federal crimes. It also works closely with state and local law enforcement agencies to address complex criminal justice issues. The DoJ's involvement in the criminal justice system is essential in ensuring that federal laws are enforced and that justice is served.

### Conclusion

The Department of Justice plays a crucial role in the American political system, particularly in the criminal justice system. Its involvement in shaping criminal justice policy is essential in upholding the law and defending the interests of the United States. The DoJ's collaboration with Congress and other federal and state agencies is crucial in addressing complex criminal justice issues and ensuring that justice is served. 





### Subsection: 20.1a The Role of Congress in Criminal Justice Policy

Congress plays a crucial role in shaping criminal justice policy in the United States. As the legislative branch of the government, it has the power to pass laws and set policies that govern the criminal justice system. This includes creating and amending federal criminal laws, determining sentencing guidelines, and allocating funding for law enforcement and criminal justice agencies.

#### The Role of Congress in Creating and Amending Federal Criminal Laws

Congress has the power to create and amend federal criminal laws. This includes determining what behaviors are considered criminal, setting penalties for those crimes, and establishing procedures for investigating and prosecuting those crimes. The Constitution gives Congress the power to make laws "necessary and proper" for carrying out its duties, which includes creating and enforcing criminal laws.

Congress also has the power to amend existing federal criminal laws. This can be done through the passage of new legislation or through the use of the sentencing guidelines set forth by the United States Sentencing Commission. These guidelines provide a framework for determining appropriate sentences for federal crimes and can be amended by Congress.

#### The Role of Congress in Determining Sentencing Guidelines

In addition to creating and amending federal criminal laws, Congress also plays a crucial role in determining sentencing guidelines. The Sentencing Reform Act of 1984 established the United States Sentencing Commission, an independent agency responsible for creating and amending sentencing guidelines for federal crimes. These guidelines provide a framework for determining appropriate sentences for federal crimes and can be amended by Congress.

Congress also has the power to set minimum and maximum sentences for federal crimes. This can be done through the passage of new legislation or through the amendment of existing laws. In some cases, Congress may also set mandatory minimum sentences for certain crimes.

#### The Role of Congress in Allocating Funding for Law Enforcement and Criminal Justice Agencies

Congress also plays a crucial role in allocating funding for law enforcement and criminal justice agencies. The Department of Justice, for example, receives the majority of its funding from Congress. This funding is used to support a variety of law enforcement and criminal justice programs, including the Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI), the Drug Enforcement Administration (DEA), and the Bureau of Prisons.

Congress also has the power to appropriate funding for specific criminal justice initiatives, such as drug treatment programs or reentry programs for prisoners. This allows Congress to shape the direction of criminal justice policy by funding programs that align with its priorities.

#### The Role of Congress in Oversight and Accountability

In addition to its role in creating and funding criminal justice policy, Congress also plays a crucial role in oversight and accountability. Congress has the power to investigate and hold hearings to examine the actions of the Department of Justice and other criminal justice agencies. This allows Congress to ensure that these agencies are operating in accordance with the law and to identify and address any issues or concerns.

Congress also has the power to hold the Attorney General and other officials accountable for their actions. This can be done through the use of subpoenas and other investigative tools, as well as through the impeachment process.

### Subsection: 20.1b The Role of Congress in Criminal Justice Reform

Congress plays a crucial role in criminal justice reform in the United States. As the legislative branch of the government, it has the power to pass laws and set policies that can address issues within the criminal justice system. This includes addressing issues of police misconduct, bail reform, and sentencing guidelines.

#### Addressing Police Misconduct

Congress has the power to pass laws and set policies that address police misconduct. This can include creating new laws that hold police officers accountable for their actions, as well as amending existing laws to strengthen accountability measures. For example, in April 2019, the governor of Maine enacted a bill that reformed the bail system, waiving fines and fees in certain cases. This was in response to concerns about the impact of bail on indigent individuals.

#### Bail Reform

Congress also plays a crucial role in bail reform. In April 2019, Maine enacted a bill that reformed the bail system, waiving fines and fees in certain cases. This was in response to concerns about the impact of bail on indigent individuals. Bail reform is an important aspect of criminal justice reform, as it can help address issues of inequality and ensure that all individuals have equal access to justice.

#### Sentencing Guidelines

Congress also plays a crucial role in setting sentencing guidelines for federal crimes. The Sentencing Reform Act of 1984 established the United States Sentencing Commission, an independent agency responsible for creating and amending sentencing guidelines for federal crimes. These guidelines provide a framework for determining appropriate sentences for federal crimes and can be amended by Congress.

Congress also has the power to set minimum and maximum sentences for federal crimes. This can be done through the passage of new legislation or through the amendment of existing laws. In some cases, Congress may also set mandatory minimum sentences for certain crimes. This allows Congress to address issues of sentencing disparities and ensure that all individuals are held accountable for their actions.

#### The Role of Congress in Criminal Justice Reform

Congress plays a crucial role in criminal justice reform in the United States. As the legislative branch of the government, it has the power to pass laws and set policies that can address issues within the criminal justice system. This includes creating and amending federal criminal laws, determining sentencing guidelines, and allocating funding for law enforcement and criminal justice agencies. By working together, Congress and the American political system can continue to make progress in criminal justice reform and ensure that all individuals have equal access to justice.





### Subsection: 20.1b The Politics of Criminal Justice Policy in Congress

The politics of criminal justice policy in Congress is a complex and ever-evolving landscape. It is shaped by a variety of factors, including public opinion, party politics, and special interest groups. In recent years, there has been a growing bipartisan consensus in favor of criminal justice reform, driven by concerns over mass incarceration, police brutality, and racial disparities in the criminal justice system.

#### The Role of Public Opinion in Criminal Justice Policy

Public opinion plays a significant role in shaping criminal justice policy in Congress. Surveys have shown that a majority of Americans support criminal justice reform, including reducing mandatory minimum sentences, increasing funding for rehabilitation programs, and addressing police misconduct. This public support has led to the passage of several criminal justice reform bills in recent years, including the First Step Act and the Second Chance Act.

#### The Role of Party Politics in Criminal Justice Policy

Party politics also play a significant role in criminal justice policy in Congress. While there is a growing bipartisan consensus in favor of criminal justice reform, there are still significant differences between the two parties. Democrats tend to favor more progressive reforms, such as legalizing marijuana and reducing prison sentences, while Republicans tend to favor tough-on-crime policies and increased funding for law enforcement.

#### The Role of Special Interest Groups in Criminal Justice Policy

Special interest groups also play a significant role in shaping criminal justice policy in Congress. Organizations such as the American Civil Liberties Union (ACLU) and the National Association for the Advancement of Colored People (NAACP) have been vocal advocates for criminal justice reform, pushing for policies that address police brutality, racial disparities, and mass incarceration. On the other hand, organizations such as the National Rifle Association (NRA) and the Fraternal Order of Police (FOP) have been vocal opponents of certain criminal justice reforms, particularly those that would limit the power of law enforcement.

#### The Role of Congress in Criminal Justice Policy

Congress plays a crucial role in shaping criminal justice policy in the United States. As the legislative branch, it has the power to pass laws and set policies that govern the criminal justice system. This includes creating and amending federal criminal laws, determining sentencing guidelines, and allocating funding for law enforcement and criminal justice agencies.

In recent years, there has been a growing bipartisan consensus in favor of criminal justice reform, driven by concerns over mass incarceration, police brutality, and racial disparities in the criminal justice system. This has led to the passage of several criminal justice reform bills, including the First Step Act and the Second Chance Act. However, there are still significant challenges and obstacles to further reform, including resistance from certain special interest groups and the need for continued public support and advocacy.
